That seems right.\n\nBut wait—what if the problem is that we're checking an old `close_at` value from a previous connection? If the connection object still exists, `connect()` is skipped entirely, which means `close_at` never gets updated. So in a scenario where a connection is reused across requests with different `CONN_MAX_AGE` settings, the old `close_at` timestamp could persist and cause the connection to close unexpectedly.\n\nEven if the setting stays constant at `CONN_MAX_AGE = None`, there's still a potential issue if the connection is established once and then reused without reinitializing the timeout value. The `connect()` method needs to be examined to see how it handles the `close_at` attribute when `CONN_MAX_AGE` is `None`.\n\nLooking at the code, `connect()` resets `close_at` each time it's called—setting it to `None` when `max_age` is `None`, or to `time.monotonic() + max_age` otherwise. This means the timeout is recalculated on every connection establishment. The real question becomes: when exactly does `connect()` get invoked, and does it happen frequently enough to prevent stale timeout values from persisting?\n\nExamining `ensure_connection()`, I see that `connect()` only executes if `self.connection is None`. Once a connection exists, subsequent calls to `ensure_connection()` skip the connection logic entirely. This creates a potential problem: if `max_age` changes between requests but the connection remains active, the `close_at` timestamp never updates to reflect the new setting. The connection could outlive its intended lifespan because the timeout was only calculated during the initial connection, not refreshed for long-lived connections.\n\nLooking more closely at the `close_at` assignment, I'm wondering if there's an edge case where it's being set to `0` rather than the intended timeout value.
